**10-60** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|OSr.23.4<br>a) the extent to which the residential building will affect the character<br>and expected use of the reserve.<br>b) the extent to which options for other possible uses of the reserve<br>are restricted by a residential unit being present.<br>c) the extent to which design and appearance of the new building<br>reflects the character of the reserve including existing buildings<br>on and adjoining the reserve.<br>d) the use of landscaping and site treatment proposed with the<br>application to soften the appearance of the development and<br>reduce the effects upon neighbouring properties.|OSr.23.5<br>Accommodation for a caretaker or manager may in some cases<br>provide a level of security that may not be possible by other<br>means. It should, however, be avoided where such<br>accommodation would adversely affect the character or<br>functions of the reserve.<br>(Refer to adjoining zone rules including: daylight, parking,<br>access, outdoor living space.)|
|OSr.24.4<br>a) the nature and scale of the activity in relation to nearby<br>residential activity, having regard to the nature of the existing and<br>permitted future uses on nearby sites.<br>b) the effect on the existing and likely foreseeable amenity of nearby<br>residential areas, particularly in relation to noise and traffic<br>generation.<br>c) the ability to remedy or mitigate any adverse effects of the activity<br>by the layout or design of the site or buildings, or by the provision<br>of landscaping or similar measures.<br>d) the topography of the site and the neighbouring areas.<br>e) the adequacy of car parking on the site, and its location in<br>relation to the neighbouring residential activities.<br>f)<br>the effectiveness, and in particular the certainty, provided by any<br>conditions or controls that might be imposed on the activity.<br>g) whether the activity is temporary, and the frequency of such<br>events (where applicable).|OSr.24.5<br>Activities which are close to residential areas and which are<br>open to the public late at night, have the potential to create<br>adverse effects which conflict with the residential nature of the<br>neighbouring areas.  Particular problems are traffic arriving and<br>leaving the premises, noise from patrons, and sometimes<br>music.  Therefore restrictions have been placed on the hours of<br>opening of such activities.<br>Away from the boundary with residentially-zoned land, there<br>are no restrictions on hours of opening, except for activities<br>which involve the sale of alcohol.  This recognises the adverse<br>environmental effects which often accompany licensed<br>premises and the patrons using them.  There are set hours for<br>off-licence premises throughout the zone.  The hours for<br>licensed premises were developed after extensive public<br>consultation under the Sale of Liquor Act and only apply to sale<br>of liquor aspects of activities.|
|OSr.25.4<br>a) the ground level required to avoid the effects of anticipated<br>flooding.<br>b) the type of inundation likely to be experienced, whether it be<br>stormwater ponding, tidal inundation, or some other combination of<br>circumstances which could lead to surface flooding.<br>c) the effects on neighbouring properties, especially with respect to<br>stormwater runoff.<br>d) Section 5.3.5 in Chapter 5 of the Nelson Tasman Land<br>Development Manual 2019.|OSr.25.5<br>The minimum ground levels set are based on the best estimates<br>from the Ministry for the Environment for likely sea level rise<br>caused by global climate change.  This takes account of<br>predicted spring tides and expected tidal surges, and their<br>consequences on stormwater ponding. Monaco is exposed to<br>wind driven tidal surge and wave set, hence an additional safety<br>margin is required there.<br>Sites below the minimum levels stated for permitted activities will<br>be assessed individually to ensure that ground or floor heights<br>are sufficient to protect the building from inundation.<br>See Rule OSr.49 (earthworks) where a site is to be filled.<br>This low lying site rule does not apply within the inundation<br>overlay - see OSr.58 (inundation overlay)|

|**OSr.26**<br>**Height of**<br>**buildings**|OSr.26.1<br>Buildings are permitted if:<br>a) in Trafalgar Park, Rutherford Park and Saxton<br>Field buildings do not exceed 18m in height, or<br>b) in the rest of the Zone, they do not exceed<br>7.5m in height<br>(see Chapter 2 Meanings of Words for definition of<br>‘Height’ [including chimneys] and ‘Height<br>measurement’).|OSr.26.2<br>not applicable|OSr.26.3<br>Activities that<br>contravene a permitted<br>condition are<br>discretionary.|
|**OSr.27**<br>**Service buildings**|OSr.27.1<br>Service buildings to be used for changing rooms,<br>ablutions, toilets or storage for sports or reserve<br>maintenance equipment are permitted if:<br>a) they do not exceed 50m2 in area.|OSr.27.2<br>not applicable|OSr.27.3<br>Activities that<br>contravene a permitted<br>condition are<br>discretionary.|
|**OSr.28**<br>**Building over or**<br>**alongside drains**<br>**(piped or open)**<br>**and water mains**|OSr.28.1<br>Structures 3metres or greater from a drain (piped<br>or open) are a permitted activity.<br>Structures closer than 3metres to a piped drain or<br>watermain are permitted provided that:<br>For drains or watermains less than or equal to<br>300mm diameter:<br>a) any structure must be located no closer than<br>1metre measured horizontally from the near side<br>of any public unsleeved water main or common<br>private or public sewer or stormwater drain, or<br>For drains or watermains greater than 300mm in<br>diameter<br>b) any structure must be located no closer than<br>1.5metre measured horizontally from the near<br>side of any public water main, or common private<br>or public sewer or stormwater drain, or<br>For drains 150mm or less in diameter<br>c) any structure may be located within 1metre or<br>directly over a common private or public drain if<br>the diameter of the pipe is 150mm or less;<br>providing that:<br>i) The length of pipe or drain built over is no<br>more than 6 meters in length; and<br>ii) There are no changes in direction or<br>junctions in the portion of the drain built<br>over; and<br>iii) The length of drain built over is relaid<br>using a continuouslengthof pipe without<br>joints,sleevedinside a 225mm diameter<br>class 4 concrete pipe; and<br>iv) There is a minimum 6metre clear length and<br>3metre clear width and 1.8metre clear<br>height at one end of the sleeve to allow<br>replacement of the pipe; and<br>v) The pipes are not water mains or<br>pressurised pipelines;<br>and in all cases<br>d) any structure may overhang the line of the pipe<br>or drain, provided the structure is cantilevered or<br>is an eave and the height to the underside of the<br>structure above ground level is not less than<br>1.8metres where the required pipe or drain is<br>greater than 150mm in diameter or width; and<br>e) any structure located within 3metres, measured<br>horizontally, from the near side of the pipe or<br>drain must have the base of the foundations<br>deeper than a line drawn at 30 degrees from the<br>horizontal from the invert (bottom) of the pipe or<br>drain (or between 30 degrees and 45 degrees if<br>the design has been certified by a suitably<br>qualified engineer)(see diagram).|OSr.28.2<br>Structures:<br>That contravene c) ii) to vi)<br>only are controlled activities<br>The matters of control are;<br>a) Physical accessibility to<br>the pipe, and<br>b) The ground/floor type<br>and design used to<br>provide quick and easy<br>removal to provide the<br>ability to access the<br>pipes for maintenance<br>and repair, and<br>c) The depth of<br>concrete/permanent<br>surface floor over the<br>pipe, and<br>d) Alternative locations for<br>the pipe and methods of<br>emplacement.|OSr.28.3<br>Buildings, swimming<br>pools or fences within<br>3m of the top of bank<br>of an open drain is a<br>discretionary activity.<br>All other activities are<br>discretionary.|

**11-26** **(26/01/08) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|OSr.29.4<br>a) any effects on the outlook of  the adjoining property.<br>b) in the case of a front fence, any impacts on the streetscape.<br>c) the design and appearance of the fence.<br>d) any positive effects on or off the site, including improved<br>privacy, security, and appearance, or the ability to screen<br>unsightly activities or objects.|OSr.29.5<br>The maximum height provides a reasonable degree of privacy or<br>security, without giving a fortress appearance, or reducing the<br>outlook of neighbours to a significant degree.<br>Fences over this height will require assessment by the resource<br>consent process, and will be judged on the purpose they serve,<br>whether they improve the appearance of the property or the<br>streetscape, and any effects (both positive and adverse) they<br>might have on neighbours.|
|OSr.30.4<br>a) the compatibility of the structure with its surroundings and its<br>appropriateness in the particular park, land, or reserve for<br>which it is proposed.<br>b) privacy for adjacent land use activities.|OSr.30.5<br>Playgrounds may or may not be a permanent fixture in a park and<br>may become obsolete as the age structure of the surrounding<br>neighbourhood population changes.<br>Daylight controls are imposed more as a means of ensuring a<br>degree of privacy is retained by adjacent sites as well as ensuring<br>that solid structures do not detract from sunlight amenity.<br>Note: all proposed playgrounds must obtain a building consent.<br>Playgrounds should also be designed to New Zealand standards<br>for playground equipment NZS5828 Part 1, 2 and 3.|
|OSr.31.4<br>a) the extent of additional shading, having regard to the size of<br>the shadow cast and the period of time the area is affected.<br>b) the nature of activities or area affected.<br>c) the effects of any shading on the public enjoyment and<br>amenity of streets and reserves.<br>d) any positive effects including possible new activities able to<br>be undertaken within the reserve.<br>e) privacy effects on adjoining properties.|OSr.31.5<br>Sports equipment is essential to many codes and accepted, even<br>expected, in sports grounds.  The slight nature of goal posts<br>makes them unlikely to cause shading or obstruction of views, and<br>they are often removed in the off-season.<br>While they may exceed adjoining zone height restrictions, they are<br>thus unlikely to cause adverse effects.<br>Sports equipment such as fitness trails can be of a more<br>substantial nature and is therefore subject to height and daylight<br>restrictions.|
|OSr.32.4<br>a) the extent of any additional shading, having regard to the<br>size of the shadow cast and the period of time an area is<br>affected.<br>b) the nature of  activities or area affected.<br>c) the effects of any shading on the public enjoyment and<br>amenity of streets and public places.|OSr.32.5<br>The daylight controls are set to allow, except where prevented by<br>topography or other natural features, at least 1.5 hours of direct<br>sunlight to every site around midday in mid-winter, or alternatively<br>2.75 hours of direct sunlight during the periods before 11am and<br>after 1.45pm at mid-winter.|

**11-30** **(12/11/12) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|OSr.41.4<br>a) the potential effects of any increase of radiofrequency exposures on<br>the health of persons near the facility.<br>b) the nature of any activities near the proposed site, having regard to<br>their susceptibility to any exposures.<br>c) the location of the site, and any potential for shielding from<br>exposures.<br>d) any cumulative effects, particularly with regard to exposure to<br>electromagnetic radiation from existing facilities in the area.|OSr.41.5<br>Certain utilities and other activities emit electromagnetic<br>radiation. These activities are perceived to be a health<br>concern to some people living near them.<br>In developing the rules on radiofrequency exposures,<br>Council has based its approach on that recommended in<br>the “National Guidelines for Managing the Effects of<br>Radiofrequency Transmitters” produced by the Ministry of<br>Health and the Ministry for the Environment in December<br>2000.  The key findings of the guidelines was that there<br>are no established health effects from exposure to<br>radiofrequency fields as long as they comply with the New<br>Zealand Standard NZS 2772.1:1999 Radiofrequency<br>Fields: Part 1 – Maximum Exposure Levels 3kHz-300GHz.<br>The Council is to be notified of location and nature of any<br>proposed facility.  Should this predictive report anticipate<br>and should exposure levels be greater than 25 percent of<br>the New Zealand Standard level, monitoring to determine<br>the actual exposure levels from the transmitting<br>installation will be required.<br>The rule contains an exception for domestic<br>radiofrequency transmitting equipment.  This is to ensure<br>that these domestic transmitting devices can be used<br>without the rules applying to their radiofrequency<br>emissions.<br>Radiofrequency emissions that exceed the exposure<br>levels set within the New Zealand Standard are to be<br>considered as non-complying activities.|
|OSr.42.4<br>a) the potential to avoid, remedy or mitigate any effects through<br>landscaping, alternative locations and structures, earth mounding,<br>colour schemes and design, or other measures.<br>b) the extent to which the utility may compromise the amenity of<br>residences in terms of lighting, noise, shading, air emissions, or<br>vibration.<br>c) any risk to health, safety or property posed by the structure or<br>activity.<br>d) how prominent the site is, taking account of any significant public or<br>private views or any significant landscapes.<br>e) the extent to which any heritage or cultural values of the site or<br>adjoining sites might be compromised by the facility.<br>f)<br>whether there would be environmental benefits in co-location of  the<br>facility with other utilities.<br>g) any cumulative effects, particularly with regard to visual impacts with<br>respect to existing facilities in the area.|OSr.42.5<br>Small scale utility buildings such as pump stations,<br>transformer boxes and phone booths are an<br>inconspicuous and inoffensive component of the utilities<br>network and are provided for as of right.  (Note: Rule<br>OSr.43 applies where these are in the road reserve.)<br>Structures larger than this (eg. reservoirs) and electricity<br>substations which have the potential to compromise the<br>residential environment are subject to the resource<br>consent process.  Consent could be declined or conditions<br>placed on the facility to ensure residential amenity, and<br>health and safety, are maintained.|

**11-36** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|OSr.43.4<br>a) any hazard presented by the utility.<br>b) the environmental setting of the structure, having regard to the<br>general locality, topography, geographical features and<br>adjacent land uses.<br>c) the visual impacts of the structure on residential or recreational<br>use of land in the vicinity, and on significant ridge lines and<br>view planes from public places, including roads.<br>d) the scale, bulk and height of the facility.<br>e) screening or landscaping of the utility and any modifications to<br>its colour and design to reduce the visual effect of the utility on<br>the surrounding environment.<br>f)<br>any constraints on placing the utility underground.<br>g)|OSr.43.5<br>Provision is made for lower pressure gas lines as permitted<br>activities, but high pressure lines which present a higher risk<br>need to be assessed as discretionary activities.<br>The electricity and telephone network is an important part of the<br>city’s infrastructure.  They, and similar facilities, are permitted if<br>located under ground. However, overhead lines have the<br>potential to detract from the residential streetscape, and have to<br>be dealt with as discretionary activities.  Maintenance and<br>operation of existing utilities is permitted in accordance with the<br>definition of "maintenance" in Chapter 2.|
|OSr.44.4<br>a) the impact any proposed lines and support structures will have<br>on the character of the area.<br>b) any alternatives considered or proposed.<br>c) the purpose of the facility and the local community it will serve.<br>d) any mitigation measures proposed.<br>e) the protection of areas of significant conservation value<br>identified in accordance with the criteria contained in DO5.1.1.|OSr.44.5<br>“Minor Upgrading” (in accordance with the definition in Chapter<br>2) is permitted in relation to larger electricity infrastructure only.<br>These utilities are primarily located on the outskirts of the city<br>and involve the higher voltage lines which are generally located<br>on the larger structures like lattice towers.  These existing<br>structures are generally visually prominent wherever they are<br>located, however it is considered that minor upgrading to the<br>extent provided for in the definition, can occur without further<br>noticeable effect.<br>Note that the definition of “minor upgrading” is set out in Chapter<br>2 – Meaning of Words.|
|OSr.45.4<br>a) the size and scale of the structures.<br>b) any visual impacts, taking account of the character of the area.<br>c) safety considerations including any effects on line of sight for<br>motorists and other road users, having regard to the usage of<br>the road.|OSr.45.5<br>This rule allows for the usual basic infrastructure in the road<br>reserve at a scale that is generally accepted by the public.  This<br>infrastructure includes street lights and other traffic equipment,<br>as well as electricity distribution boxes and telephone booths.<br>The rule is not restricted to network utility operators, to regulate<br>bus shelters and street vendors.<br>The Council (and the NZ Transport Agency in the case of State<br>Highways) has an additional control as the owner of the land, in<br>respect of who goes there and under what circumstances.<br>Telecommunications and electricity operators have certain rights<br>to put works on roads (under the Telecommunications Act 1987,<br>section 15, and Electricity Act 1992, section 24).  The Council<br>will be guided when it prescribes conditions under those Acts, by<br>the matters contemplated in these rules.|
|OSr.46.4<br>a) the matters in Chapter 4 of the Nelson Tasman Land<br>Development Manual 2019.<br>b) the noise and air emissions from the road, taking account of<br>the nature of nearby activities.<br>c) any implications for traffic and pedestrian safety, both positive<br>and adverse.<br>d) any proposals to mitigate the adverse effects of the road,<br>particularly by screening for noise and visual impacts.<br>e) any impacts on communities eg. whether the road would<br>divide a neighbourhood.<br>f)<br>any opportunities to provide views and vistas from the road.|OSr.46.5<br>New roads and realignments and upgrading work on existing<br>roads are generally dealt with by way of designations in the<br>Plan.  The designation procedure provides an opportunity for the<br>public or affected persons to have a say on the proposals and<br>for the environmental effects to be scrutinised.  Where new<br>roads have not been dealt with by designation, this rule provides<br>the opportunity for public input via the resource consent process<br>if the road is to be a major traffic carrier, or the usual design<br>standards are not being followed.|

**11-38** **(09/10/18) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|OSr.49A.4<br>In considering applications for landfills, Council will restrict its discretion to the<br>matters identified in OSr.49A.3 provided that the application does not<br>contravene a restricted discretionary condition|OSr.49A.5<br>Landfill development and operation are restricted discretionary<br>activities.  While landfills generally involve earthworks and modify the<br>shape of the land, their primary purpose is the disposal of solid waste.<br>The concentration of solid waste, often containing contaminants,<br>within landfill sites, increases the potential for adverse effects on the<br>environment beyond that associated with normal earthworks.<br>Additional adverse effects may include generation of leachate,<br>discharge of gas, geotechnical issues such as poor bearing strength<br>and settlement, and contamination of the land itself making it<br>unsuitable for other uses.<br>Some landfills operate as cleanfill sites, accepting only material that<br>does not undergo any physical, chemical, or biological transformations<br>likely to cause adverse environmental effects, or health effects, once it<br>is placed in the ground.  Cleanfill operations are clearly preferable to<br>activities accepting hazardous or potentially hazardous materials, and<br>are more likely to be granted resource consent.<br>Discharge to water and air from material within landfill sites also<br>require separate regional resource consents.|


|OSr504<br>. .<br>a) effectsonvisualamenity<br>.<br>b) thematterscontainedinAppendix7(guideforsubdivisionand<br>structuresinthelandscapeoverlays)<br>.<br>c) thematterscontainedinAppendix9(landscapecomponentsand<br>views)<br>.<br>d) methodstoavoid remedyormitigateadverseeffects<br>, .<br>e) landstability soilerosionandsedimentationeffects<br>, .<br>f) vegetation clearance and planting - extent, method and timing .|OSr505<br>. .<br>Therulepreservesthevisualamenityoftheoverlays Cutor<br>.<br>fillof12mispermittedastheadverseeffectsareconsidered<br>.<br>tobeacceptable unlessassociatedwithconstructionof<br>,<br>roadsandtracks whichleaveextensivelinearscarsonthe<br>,<br>landscape Activitiesthatdonotcomplywithpermitted<br>.<br>conditionsarediscretionary TheOverlayismanagedto<br>.<br>preserveopencharacter<br>.<br>NotethatEarthworks SoilDisturbanceandVegetation<br>,<br>Clearanceareregulatedinotherrules whichmayimpose<br>,<br>additionalregulationonparticularactivities<br>.|
|---|---|
|OSr.51.4<br>a) the mandatory matters and good practice guidance contained in<br>the Nelson Tasman Land Development Manual 2019.<br>b) that the location of the building or development does not impede<br>the route and construction of any future road or utility services<br>required to service the site or surrounding sites with potential for<br>residential development.<br>c) whether road access and reticulated services are able to be<br>provided to the site and any surrounding site with potential for<br>residential development from any other practical route.<br>d) whether the building or development can provide for on site<br>servicing for the building or development in accordance with<br>FWr.12, FWr.14, FWr.25 and FWr.29.<br>e) the strategic planning programme for servicing sites within the<br>district.|OSr.51.5<br>The Services Overlay is the area shown on the Planning<br>Maps where the existing stormwater drainage or wastewater<br>system is not available (for example, because of the relative<br>levels) or has insufficient capacity to accept more discharges<br>or new connections.<br>Under its Long Term Plan, the Council has a programme for<br>progressive upgrading of the stormwater in the Services<br>Overlay. Until that upgrading takes place, building will be<br>discretionary.<br>If a developer proposes a short term access, drainage or<br>water supply method that is not consistent with the Long<br>Term Plan, the effects of this on the environment and the<br>Long Term Plan (especially any compromising effect on the<br>overall development of the City systems) will be assessed<br>when a resource consent application is considered.<br>Gravity fed systems are preferred because these have lower<br>maintenance costs and are more reliable.<br>The Services Overlay is also used to ensure that practical<br>road access and the extension of services from one property<br>to another which has potential for residential development is<br>maintained.  New buildings or extensions will not be<br>permitted in locations where this hinders or prevents the only<br>practical route for a future road or reticulated services to<br>serve the site and adjoining site with potential for residential<br>development.<br>Use of on site servicing within the Industrial Zone Services<br>Overlay is discouraged, and the application would be<br>considered as a discretionary activity.<br>Note: The capacity of the stormwater drain or wastewater<br>network means the capacity of the length of the drain from<br>the site to, and including, its outfall to a water body, coastal<br>water or treatment facility.|

|RUr.28.4<br>a)<br>any proposed activity to which any additional residential units are<br>related.<br>b)<br>the matters in the Nelson Tasman Land Development Manual 2019.<br>c)<br>the extent to which the land is subject to natural hazards, including<br>inundation, floodpaths, fault areas and slope risk areas, and whether<br>any risks can be remedied or mitigated.<br>d)<br>the pattern of subdivision and how it relates to the desired<br>environmental outcomes and amenity values for the locality.<br>e)<br>the extent that provision is made for the actual and legal protection of<br>significant natural features or heritage items, and avoids or mitigates<br>any significant changes to the landscape or amenity values of the<br>area.<br>f)<br>the extent to which any other adverse effects of the proposed<br>development are avoided, remedied or mitigated, including<br>temporary effects associated with construction.<br>g)<br>any actual or potential effects including cumulative effects on the<br>character of the area.<br>h)<br>the significance of any building used for an activity not permitted as<br>of right and whether it will lead to intensification of the activity.<br>i)<br>the scale of the structure and activity associated with it.<br>j)<br>the protection of areas of significant conservation value shown in the<br>areas of significant conservation value overlay or identified in<br>accordance with the criteria contained in section Policy DO5.1.1.<br>k)<br>the likelihood of an activity being established adjacent to the property<br>which may impact on the amenity of any dwelling.<br>l)<br>the likelihood of any dwelling being erected on an adjacent property<br>which may be affected by the non-compliance including matters<br>such as daylight admission, noise, disturbance and smell.<br>m) the potential for spread of fire from or to adjacent properties given<br>present and potential activities which may be carried out in the area.<br>n)<br>the extent to which any building erected close to a road is likely to<br>affect traffic visibility and rural character.<br>o)<br>any other fire mitigation measures proposed other than provision of<br>defensible space.<br>p)<br>in buildings without sprinkler systems, whether a lesser amount of<br>water storage than 45,000 litres, or no storage is required, for rural<br>fire fighting purposes for a particular building, due to proximity to a<br>water source, as assessed by a New Zealand Fire Service Fire Risk<br>Management Officer.  Confirmation should be provided in writing.<br>q)  the extent of compliance with the New Zealand Fire Service<br>Firefighting Water Supplies Code of Practice (SNZ PAS 4509:2008).<br>r)      the effects on road and walkway/cycleway connectivity where the<br>building is sited on, or close, to an indicative road, or<br>walkway/cycleway shown, or described on a Structure Plan.|<br> <br>RUr.28.5<br>Provides for one residential unit as of right on any one<br>site, and other buildings as appropriate and any further<br>residential units proposed to be considered on their<br>merits.<br>All buildings have to provide a minimum buffer which,<br>when coupled with the buffer area required on adjacent<br>properties provides for adequate protection against<br>amenity and hazard effects.<br>Provision is made for detached non habitable buildings<br>such as garages, sheds, barns, utility buildings, and<br>storerooms to be located within 10m of a boundary<br>provided they are less than 40m2 in area.<br>Buildings and any further residential units have to fit all<br>the criteria as if it were a subdivided site.  This avoids<br>people taking a backdoor route to further subdivision<br>once a second residential unit is established, arguing that<br>subdivision has no physical effects on the environment or<br>the infrastructure.<br>Water storage is required with all new buildings in the<br>Small Holdings and Rural Zones in recognition of the<br>increasing demands that new developments will make on<br>rural waterways.<br>During periods where minimum flow is reached all<br>abstractions will be required to cease.  Use of stored<br>water will be necessary during these times. Individual<br>households are responsible for ensuring that their stored<br>drinking water is potable.<br>The following district wide policies are relevant to this rule:<br>DO18.2.2 (water storage)<br>DO18.4.10 (permitted abstractions)<br>It is expected that where a building is to be located on, or<br>close to, an indicative road or walkway/cycleway, the<br>developer will provide sufficient assurance that the<br>building will not compromise the achievement of future<br>connectivity generally in the alignment.|

**12-32** **(12/11/12) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|RUr.29.4<br>a)<br>the ground level required to avoid the effects of anticipated<br>flooding.<br>b)<br>the type of inundation likely to be experienced, whether it be<br>stormwater, ponding, tidal inundation, or some other<br>combination of circumstances which could lead to surface<br>flooding.<br>c)<br>the effects on neighbouring properties, especially with respect to<br>stormwater runoff.<br>d)<br>section 5.3.5, Chapter 5 of the Nelson Tasman Land<br>Development Manual 2019.|RUr.29.5<br>The minimum ground levels set are based on the best<br>estimates from the Ministry for the Environment for likely sea<br>level rise caused by global climate change.  This takes<br>account of predicted spring tides and expected tidal surges,<br>and their consequent effects on stormwater ponding.<br>Sites below the minimum levels stated for permitted activities<br>will be assessed individually to ensure that ground or floor<br>heights are sufficient to protect the building from inundation.<br>See Rule RUr.27 (earthworks) where a site is to be filled.<br>This Rule relates to minimum ground and floor levels for<br>hazard mitigation purposes. It does not relate to minimum<br>floor levels arising from the requirements of the Building Act.<br>The Building Act may require height additional to that<br>specified in this rule (to achieve clearance to ground, crawl<br>space and other requirements).|



**Nelson Resource Management Plan (12/07/21)** **12-33**


|Item|Permitted|Controlled|Discretionary/Non-<br>complying|
|---|---|---|---|
|**RUr.31A**<br>**Building over**<br>**or alongside**<br>**drains (piped**<br>**or open) and**<br>**water mains**|<br>RUr.31A.1<br>Structures 3metres or greater from a drain (piped or open)<br>are a permitted activity.<br>Structures closer than 3metres to a piped drain or watermain<br>are permitted provided that:<br>For drains or watermains less than or equal to 300mm<br>diameter:<br>a) any structure must be located no closer than 1metre<br>measured horizontally from the near side of any public<br>unsleeved water main or common private or public sewer<br>or stormwater drain, or<br>For drains or watermains greater than 300mm in diameter<br>b) any structure must be located no closer than 1.5metre<br>measured horizontally from the near side of any public<br>water main, or common private or public sewer or<br>stormwater drain, or<br>For drains 150mm or less in diameter<br>c) any structure may be located within 1metre or directly<br>over a common private or public drain if the diameter of<br>the pipe is 150mm or less; providing that:<br>i) The length of pipe or drain built over is no more<br>than 6meters in length; and<br>ii) There are no changes in direction or junctions in<br>the portion of the drain built over; and<br>iii) The length of drain built over is relaid using a<br>continuouslengthof pipe without joints,sleeved<br>inside a 225mm diameter class 4 concrete pipe;<br>and<br>iv) There is a minimum 6metre clear length and 3metre<br>clear width and 1.8metre clear height at one end of<br>the sleeve to allow replacement of the pipe; and<br>v) The pipes are not water mains or pressurised<br>pipelines;<br>and in all cases<br>d) any structure may overhang the line of the pipe or drain,<br>provided the structure is cantilevered or is an eave and<br>the height to the underside of the structure above ground<br>level is not less than 1.8metres where the required pipe or<br>drain is greater than 150mm in diameter or width; and<br>e) any structure located within 3metres, measured<br>horizontally, from the near side of the pipe or drain must<br>have the base of the foundations deeper than a line drawn<br>at 30 degrees from the horizontal from the invert (bottom)<br>of the pipe or drain (or between 30 degrees and 45<br>degrees if the design has been certified by a suitably<br>qualified engineer)(see diagram).|RUr.31A.2<br>Structures:<br>That contravene c) ii) to<br>vi) only are controlled<br>activities<br>The matters of control<br>are;<br>a) Physical accessibility<br>to the pipe, and<br>b) The ground/floor type<br>and design used to<br>provide quick and<br>easy removal to<br>provide the ability to<br>access the pipes for<br>maintenance and<br>repair, and<br>c) The depth of<br>concrete/permanent<br>surface floor over the<br>pipe, and<br>d) Alternative locations<br>for the pipe and<br>methods of<br>emplacement.|RUr.31A.3<br>Buildings,<br>swimming pools or<br>fences within<br>3metres of the top<br>of bank of an open<br>drain is a<br>discretionary<br>activity.<br>All other activities<br>are discretionary.|
|**RUr.32**<br>**Buildings -**<br>**height**|RUr.32.1<br>Maximum height -12m.<br>(see Chapter 2 Meanings of Words for definition of ‘Height’<br>[including chimneys] and ‘Height measurement’).|RUr.32.2<br>not applicable|RUr.32.3<br>Activities that<br>contravene a<br>permitted condition<br>are discretionary.|
|**RUr.33**<br>**Site coverage**<br>**for**<br>**structures,**<br>**storage, and**<br>**utility yard**<br>**areas**|<br>RUr.33.1<br>Construction or alteration of structures and paving of land with<br>impervious surfacing (including driveways, paths and yards) is<br>permitted if no more than 2500m2 of any site is covered by<br>structures or impervious surfaces (other than structures used for<br>handling animals or plants).|RUr.33.2<br>not applicable|RUr.33.3<br>Activities that<br>contravene the<br>conditions for<br>permitted activities<br>are discretionary.|



**12-34** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|RUr.31A.4<br>a) the nature of the structure and whether<br>access to the pipe or drain can be<br>maintained<br>b) any measures taken to ensure that<br>replacement of the pipe or drain can be<br>undertaken.<br>c) the nature of the pipe or drain, taking<br>into account materials of construction<br>and any bends or joints.<br>The accessibility of the pipework or drain<br>and the ease by which it could be<br>extracted.|RUr.31A.5<br>Limiting access to pipes and drains means that repair and maintenance may be<br>very costly and may even result in pipes or drains having to be relocated.  This<br>rule seeks to preserve access to all pipes or drains where off-site facilities are<br>likely to be affected.<br>In response to frequent requests for carports to be built over pipes, this has<br>been made a permitted activity provided the carport does not become enclosed<br>and the depth of any concrete floor does not exceed 150mm.<br>However, a common problem arises when the carport is later closed in without<br>Council’s knowledge.  An encumbrance on the title will alert landowners to the<br>location of the pipe or drain and remind them that access to the pipe or drain is<br>to remain unimpeded and all costs associated with obtaining access, (including<br>the removal and reinstatement of floors or walls) are the responsibility of the<br>landowner.<br>Provided the encumbrance is registered on the title, carports built over drains or<br>pipes, do not require a resource consent.<br>Alternative techniques for ensuring access for maintenance and repair purposes<br>may be considered on a case by case basis through the resource consent<br>process.<br>Table 3-4, section 3 of the NCC Land Development Manual 2010 provides<br>techniques which allow the construction of structures over drains in some other<br>limited circumstances.<br>At the time that application is made for building consent, a request shall be<br>made in writing to waive the rule relating to “Building over or alongside drains,<br>pipes and water mains” where one of these Techniques is proposed to apply.<br>**Diagram referred to in RUr.31A.1a:**|
|RUr.32.4<br>a)<br>the extent to which taller buildings will<br>impact on the rural character of the<br>area.<br>b)<br>the impact on amenities of adjacent<br>properties.|RUr.32.5<br>The height represents a scale which is acceptable in human terms and within which<br>most dwellings (including two-storey dwellings) and structures can be accommodated.<br>Discretion is reserved to consent to higher structures when compliance is not a viable<br>alternative and consent is consistent with the purpose of the Act.  Buildings over 12m or<br>averaging over 12m on sloping ground are discretionary.|
|RUr.33.4<br>a)<br>any actual or potential effects<br>including cumulative effects on the<br>long term  life supporting capacity of<br>the soil.<br>b)<br>the quality of the land and any<br>mitigating features (such as previous<br>contamination) which has affected the<br>land.<br>c)<br>effects on the local amenity of the<br>area.<br>d)<br>effects on the character of the area.|<br>RUr.33.5<br>This rule is primarily directed to protection of the rural amenity values and the<br>sustainable use of rural soil. It provides adequate work space for most activities which<br>are not related to the land, but requires that any other activity which is space demanding<br>and not related to the land to apply for resource consent and allow each case to be<br>considered on its merits.|

**12-36** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|RUr.34.4<br>a)<br>the likelihood of any dwelling being located adjacent to the site, given the<br>present and likely pattern of development in the area.<br>b)<br>any likely shading effects on adjacent property.<br>c)<br>the potential for spread of fire from or to adjacent properties given present<br>and potential activities which may be carried out in the area.<br>d)<br>the extent to which any plantings will shade any public road<br>e)<br>danger to life and property as a result of falling trees.|RUr.34.5<br>This rule provides that shelterbelts should be kept at least 10m from a<br>property boundary except in the Small Holdings Area.  Here<br>shelterbelts located on southern boundaries, where they are most<br>likely to shade adjoining properties, are subject to additional recession<br>plane controls.<br>While shelterbelts are established and important features in rural<br>areas, when located close to boundaries they can have a significant<br>effect on the productivity and enjoyment of adjoining properties.<br>Effects on neighbouring properties can include but are not limited to a<br>reduction in soil fertility, reduction in growth rates of adjoining pasture<br>and crops, and shading of neighbouring houses and living areas,<br>creating a feeling of enclosure.<br>Shading effects are most likely to occur where stands of tall trees are<br>located adjacent to the northern boundary of smaller and more<br>intensely developed properties.|
|RUr.35.4<br>Refer to Appendix 10.|RUr.35.5<br>Refer to Appendix 10.|

**12-38** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|RUr.41.4<br>a)<br>the potential effects of any increase of<br>radiofrequency exposures on the health of<br>persons near the facility.<br>b)<br>the nature of any activities near the proposed<br>site, having regard to their susceptibility to any<br>exposures.<br>c)<br>the location of the site, and any potential for<br>shielding from exposures.<br>d)<br>any cumulative effects, particularly with regard to<br>exposure to electro magnetic radiation from<br>existing facilities in the area.|RUr.41.5<br>Certain utilities and other activities emit electromagnetic radiation. These activities are<br>perceived to be a health concern to some people living near them.<br>In developing the rules on radiofrequency exposures, Council has based its approach on that<br>recommended in the “National Guidelines for Managing the Effects of Radiofrequency<br>Transmitters” produced by the Ministry of Health and the Ministry for the Environment in<br>December 2000.  The key findings of the guidelines was that there are no established health<br>effects from exposure to radiofrequency fields as long as they comply with the New Zealand<br>Standard NZS 2772.1:1999 Radiofrequency Fields: Part 1 – Maximum Exposure Levels<br>3kHz-300GHz.<br>The Council is to be notified of location and nature of any proposed facility.  Should this<br>predictive report anticipate and should exposure levels be greater than 25% of the New<br>Zealand Standard level, monitoring to determine the actual exposure levels from the<br>transmitting installation will be required.<br>The rule contains an exception for domestic radiofrequency transmitting equipment.  This is to<br>ensure that these domestic transmitting devices can be used without the rules applying to their<br>radiofrequency emissions.<br>Radiofrequency emissions that exceed the exposure levels set within the New Zealand<br>Standard are to be considered as non-complying activities.|
|RUr.42.4<br>a)<br>any potential or cumulative effects on health of<br>persons from exposure to electro-magnetic fields<br>from existing facilities in the area.<br>b)<br>any effects of structures and lines including<br>noise, visual impact and physical risk.|RUr.42.5<br>A number of high voltage transmission lines traverse the Rural Zone.  These locations are<br>shown on the Planning Maps.  A separation distance between lines and housing is appropriate<br>for a range of reasons including physical aspects and possible long term health exposure risks<br>which may affect some people living in close proximity to major lines.  This rule provides an<br>opportunity for evaluation of such effects when new housing is proposed in proximity to these<br>lines (see also RUr.44 for new lines). All buildings, structures, plant and excavations are also<br>required to comply with NZECP 34 2001 for Electrical Safe Distances.  In some situations,<br>depending upon the length of span and operating temperature of the line, a greater separation<br>distance than is provided for in the rule may be necessary.  Advice should be sought from the<br>line owner.|

|**RUr.47**<br>**Noise**<br>Advisory Note: <br>Rule RUr.47 does<br>not apply to noise<br>in relation to<br>plantation forestry<br>activities as these<br>are regulated<br>under regulations<br>22-35 of the<br>Resource<br>Management<br>(National<br>Environmental<br>Standards for<br>Plantation<br>Forestry)<br>Regulations 2018.|<br> <br>RUr.47.1<br>a)<br>Noise levels from Rural Areas when measured<br>at or  within any site in a Residential Zone must<br>not exceed:<br>Day Time<br>55 dBA (L10)<br>Other Times<br>45 dBA (L10)<br>75 dBA (Lmax)<br>Day Time means 7am-10pm Monday to Friday, and<br>9am-10pm Saturdays, Sundays and Public Holidays.<br>b)<br>Noise levels measured at or within the notional<br>boundary of any rural dwelling other than any<br>dwelling on the site from which the noise is<br>being generated, must not exceed:<br>**Day Time**<br>55 dBA (L10)<br>**Other Times**<br>45 dBA (L10)<br>75 dBA (Lmax)<br>Day Time means 6am-10pm Monday to Sundays.<br>For Part (b) of this Rule, all noise from normal rural<br>activities shall be excluded, including:<br>i) mobile horticultural and agricultural equipment, and<br>ii) temporary forest and tree harvesting activities, and<br>iii) animals, except when associated with intensive<br>commercial livestock farming and animal boarding<br>activities, and<br>iv) bird scares and hail canons.<br>c)<br>All measurements and assessment in<br>accordance with NZS6801:1991 and<br>NZS6802:1991.|<br>RUr.47.2<br>not applicable|RUr.47.3<br>Activities that contravene a permitted condition are<br>discretionary.|

|RUr.48.4<br>Assessment Criteria in Appendix 21.|RUr.48.5<br>See Appendix 21.|

|RUr.49A.4<br>a)<br>the matters  in the Nelson Tasman Land Development Manual 2019.<br>b)<br>that the location of the building does not impede the route and<br>construction of any future road or utility services required to service the site<br>or surrounding sites with potential for residential development.<br>c) whether road access and reticulated services are able to be provided to<br>the site and any surrounding site with potential for residential development<br>from any other practical route.<br>d) whether the building  can provide for on site servicing for the building in<br>accordance with FWr.12, FWr.14, FWr.25 and FWr.29.<br>e)<br>the strategic planning programme for servicing sites within the district.|<br>RUr.49A.5<br>The Services Overlay is the area shown on the Planning Maps where<br>the existing water supply, stormwater drainage or wastewater system<br>is not available (for example, because of the relative levels) or has<br>insufficient capacity to accept more discharges or new connections.<br>Under its Long Term Plan, the Council has a programme for<br>progressive upgrading of the stormwater, wastewater, water and<br>roading networks in the City.  Until that upgrading takes place, building<br>will be discretionary.<br>If a developer proposes a short term access, drainage or water supply<br>method that is not consistent with the Long Term Plan, the effects of<br>this on the environment and the Long Term Plan (especially any<br>compromising effect on the overall development of the City systems)<br>will be assessed when a resource consent application is considered.<br>Gravity fed systems are preferred because these have lower<br>maintenance costs and are more reliable.<br>The Services Overlay is also used to ensure that practical road<br>access and the extension of services from one property to another<br>which has potential for development is maintained.  Erection or<br>extension of buildings will not be permitted in locations where this<br>hinders or prevents the only practical route for a future road or<br>reticulated services to serve the site and adjoining site with potential<br>for development.<br>Use of on site servicing within the Rural Zone Services Overlay is<br>discouraged, and the application would be considered as a<br>discretionary activity.<br>Note: The capacity of the stormwater drain or wastewater network<br>means the capacity of the length of the drain from the site to, and<br>including, its outfall to a water body, coastal water or treatment facility.|

**12- 48** **(01/09/04) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|RUr.68.4 - RUr.72.4<br>Refer to Assessment Criteria on preceding page|RUr.71.5<br>Heritage Trees are trees for which retention is considered essential.  Landscape Trees<br>are trees for which retention is considered important.<br>Tighter controls are placed on Heritage Trees recognising that they are of greater<br>significance to the community than the Landscape Trees.<br>(note: section 330 of the Act provides for emergency works, such as the removal of<br>trees threatening life or property, but in strictly defined circumstance and by certain<br>persons or agencies.  Consent for such works can be applied for retrospectively<br>(section 330A)).  Some latitude is provided for the removal of trees in the road reserve<br>if it can be shown it places an unreasonable restriction on the roading network,<br>recognising that the primary purpose of the road reserve is transportation.|
|RUr.68.4 - RUr.72.4<br>Refer to Assessment Criteria on preceding page|RUr.72.5<br>Local Trees are of noteworthy interest, and are not as significant as either Heritage or<br>Landscape Trees.  Retention and protection is encouraged.  A week’s notice prior to<br>removal of a Local Tree allows the Council the opportunity to negotiate with the owner<br>if it desires.  Written notice also allows the Council to update its list of Local Trees.|
|RUr.73.4<br>a)<br>the effects of the building on the activities of existing<br>quarries.<br>b)<br>the effects of the building on future quarry activities.<br>c)<br>the efficient present and future extraction of the rock<br>resource.|RUr.73.5<br>The Potential Quarries Overlay is the area surrounding the points identified on the<br>Planning Maps where there is a significant rock resource suitable for quarrying. The<br>rock resource is of great value to the community generally.<br>New buildings are incompatible with quarries in that they cover the resource and make<br>it physically more difficult to access. Residential units are especially incompatible with<br>the effects of quarrying, including noise, vibration dust and the effects of heavy<br>machinery and transport both on site and on nearby roads. It is likely that residents will<br>find these effects unacceptable and this will tend to inhibit the utilisation of the rock<br>resource. This Plan therefore limits the establishment of new residential units in the<br>vicinity.|
|RUr.74.4<br>See Schedule Sch.Q.|RUr.74.5<br>See Schedule Sch.Q.<br>The schedules for this Zone follow after the Rule Table|

|RUr.76.4<br>See Schedule Sch.S.|RUr.76.5<br>See Schedule Sch.S.<br>The schedules for this Zone follow after the Rule Table.|

13)_<br>_Continued overleaf..._|<br> <br>RUr.78.5<br>Specific rules apply to subdivision activities proposed within the<br>Services, Coastal Environment, Conservation, Natural Hazard or<br>Heritage Overlay (see Rule RUr.79 to RUr.84).<br>Subdivision is a controlled activity so that conditions can be<br>imposed on the development to ensure a satisfactory standard of<br>amenity, the minimisation of adverse effects and that the site can<br>be adequately serviced.<br>In the case of a subdivision created only for access or for a network<br>utility the plan provides that minimum lot sizes and requirements of<br>access and services do not apply provided that the title of the lot<br>records that it was created purely for access or for a network utility<br>service and it may not comply with requirements for other uses.<br>The controls also enable consideration to be had to the<br>development potential of nearby land, so that the level of servicing<br>provided is appropriate to the likely future demand in the area.  This<br>allows consideration of future roading patterns, and demands on<br>other services, to avoid the need for costly and disruptive<br>upgrading later.<br>The Act states the preservation of the natural character of the river<br>and coastal margins to be matters of national importance.  This<br>includes public access along these, and protection of areas of<br>significant indigenous flora and the habitats of indigenous fauna,<br>contained within them. It provides a number of mechanisms to<br>achieve this protection including the taking of esplanade reserves<br>and strips.<br>The river and coastal margins on the Planning Maps and in<br>Appendix 6, Table 6.2 (riparian or coastal areas with priority<br>values), have been identified as containing riparian values in<br>accord with section 229 of the Act.  In some cases riparian<br>protection already exists, or special circumstances exist, which<br>make the acquisition of esplanade reserves or strips unnecessary.<br>In other cases a lesser width or an alternative way of achieving<br>riparian protection may be more appropriate.  In these situations, a<br>discretionary consent application is needed, in order to depart from<br>the standards set out for a normal subdivision.<br>Where a subdivision is discretionary, and the allotment adjoins any<br>river or the coastal margin, then an esplanade reserve or strip may<br>be required as a condition of the subdivision consent, having<br>regard to the values identified in Appendix 6, Table 6.1 (riparian and<br>coastal margins with identified riparian values).<br>In the Small Holdings Area an average lot size of 3 ha with a<br>minimum lot size of 2ha is provided for as a controlled activity.  With<br>the exception of lots created for access or solely for the provision of<br>network utilities, lots of less than 1ha become a non-complying<br>activity.<br>_Continued overleaf..._|

In Marsden Valley Schedule I (Eastern Rural Zone –<br>Higher Density Small Holdings Area only, Chapter 7 Residential<br>Zone) the Marsden Hills (Schedule V) and Enner Glynn (Schedule<br>W) and Ngawhatu Higher Density Small Holdings Area, the<br>average lot size is 1 ha with the minimum size 2,000m2. This area<br>provides a transition between residential development and the rural<br>land beyond, and with development sensitive to landscape values<br>as it relates to the more elevated land below the Barnicoat Range.<br>The low minimum subdivision area enables discrete<br>enclaves/clusters of development to be designed with large open<br>space/landscaped areas separating the enclaves. The extent of<br>geotechnical constraints in the area will result in quite defined and<br>separated enclaves of development.<br>In Marsden Valley (Schedule I, Chapter 7, Residential Zone) the<br>western Rural Zone – Higher Density Small Holdings Area has a<br>site size requirement of 6,000m2 average and 2,000m2 minimum<br>with a requirement for reticulated services.  This recognises that<br>this area is surrounded by Residential zoning and is therefore not<br>located in a rural or rural to residential transition environment.<br>The papakainga provisions are included in the Plan in recognition<br>of the special needs relating to land held in multiple ownership.<br>Considerable flexibility from the normal density and other rules is<br>provided in Sch.P.  In order to avoid the papakainga provisions<br>being used to get around normal density requirements in the Rural<br>Zone, subdivision of land that is part of a papakainga development<br>has been made a prohibited activity.|

|---|---|---|---|
|**RUr.80**<br>**Subdivision**<br>**within the**<br>**Landscape**<br>**Overlay**|RUr.80.1<br>Not a<br>permitted<br>activity in this<br>Overlay.|<br>RUr.80.2<br>Subdivision of land within the Landscape Overlay and detailed in<br>Appendix 9 (landscape components and views) is a controlled<br>activity if:<br>a)<br>it meets the standards in rule RUr.78 (subdivision - general),<br>and<br>b)<br>it is accompanied by a landscape assessment by an<br>appropriately qualified person which takes into account the<br>guidelines in Appendix 7 (guide for subdivision and structures<br>in the landscape overlay) and identifies any areas on which<br>building should not occur because the landscape effects of a<br>building cannot be mitigated in that area..<br>Control reserved over:<br>i)<br>the matters in rule RUr.78 (subdivision - general), with<br>particular regard to the visual impacts of the subdivision<br>and the likely structures that will be built on the<br>subdivided land, and<br>ii)<br>retention of existing vegetation and other site features,<br>and<br>iii)<br>extent and form of earthworks, and<br>iv) the alignment and location of roads, the width of<br>carriageways and planting of berms, and the alignment<br>of road accesses with the land contours, and<br>v)<br>the size, shape and orientation of allotments, and<br>vi) the location of any building site, and<br>vii) location and design of utilities, and<br>viii) location and type of planting for amenity and restoration,<br>ix) changes to landform or landscape features, and<br>x)<br>views from roads andpublic reserves.|RUr.80.3<br> Any subdivision that contravenes a controlled<br>standard is a discretionary activity if it is for the<br>purposes of a network utility.  Any other<br>subdivision that contravenes a controlled<br>standard is discretionary if:<br>a)<br>every allotment (other than an access<br>allotment) complies with the mandatory<br>matters relating to stormwater and<br>wastewater in Chapters 5 & 6 of the<br>Nelson Tasman Land Development<br>Manual 2019, and<br>b)<br>the net area of allotments is greater than<br>1 ha in the Small Holdings Areas except<br>where lots have been created for the<br>purpose of access or network utilities.<br>Any subdivision in the Rural Zone or Low<br>Density Small Holdings Area located between<br>The Glen Road (including all areas east of<br>The Glen Road) and Whangamoa Saddle<br>which does not meet the controlled activity<br>minimum lot sizes is a non-complying activity,<br>except where lots have been created for the<br>purpose of access or network utilities.05/01<br>Subdivision of land that is part of a<br>papakainga development as defined Sch.P is<br>a prohibited activity.|
|**RUr.81**<br>**Subdivision**<br>**within Heritage**<br>**Overlays**|RUr.81.1<br>Not a<br>permitted<br>activity in this<br>Overlay.|<br>RUr.81.2<br>not applicable|RUr.81.3<br>Subdivision of land containing any heritage<br>feature is a discretionary activity.<br>Any subdivision in the Rural Zone or Low<br>Density Small Holdings Area located between<br>The Glen Road (including all areas east of<br>The Glen Road) and Whangamoa Saddle<br>which does not  meet the controlled activity<br>minimum lot sizes is a non-complying activity,<br>except where lots have been created for the<br>purpose of access or network utilities.05/01|

**12-76** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


|AssessmentCriteria|Explanation|
|---|---|
|RUr.85.4<br>a)<br>whether the disposal of storm water or  wastewater from the site, or<br>supply of water, can be done effectively without risk to human health or<br>the environment, natural hazards or adjoining properties.<br>b)<br>the matters in the Nelson Tasman Land Development Manual 2019.<br>c)<br>the assessment matters in Rule RUr.78.4 (Subdivision: General).<br>d)<br>the timing of the development in relation to the availability of roading<br>and servicing connections.<br>e)<br>the extent to which the development is provided with services of<br>adequate capacity to serve the future development level of the site and<br>surrounding sites.<br>f)<br> the financial costs to Council (including operation and maintenance<br>costs) of providing water and wastewater services to the development.<br>g)<br>whether the building or development can provide for on site servicing<br>for the building or development in accordance with FWr.12, FWr.14,<br>FWr.25 and FWr.29.|<br>RUr.85.5<br>Subdivision in the Services Overlay provides specific services related<br>assessment matters in addition to those that would be applied to a<br>subdivision consent application under RUr.79 General.  For subdivision<br>in the Services Overlay, subdivision consent is only required under<br>RUr.85, unless any other overlays also apply to the site.<br>Subdivision in the Services Overlay is a restricted discretionary activity<br>where it proposes to connect to public reticulated  This provides a<br>limitation on the range and type of conditions that can be imposed,<br>relevant to particular sites and circumstances.  It also allows the option of<br>declining a subdivision if the site or the development is unsuitable or<br>servicing of the site is considered insufficient or unsustainable for<br>Council.<br>On site servicing will be considered as a discretionary activity.<br>The areas defined on the Planning Maps include areas where the<br>provision of services to subdivisions is not straightforward or where the<br>allotment size, topography and orientation mean that it will be difficult to<br>accommodate on site wastewater treatment and disposal.  There may<br>be constraints on the capacity of existing systems, or the area may need<br>filling in order to get the necessary fall for stormwater or wastewater<br>drainage. Special regard also has to be had to the roading pattern, to<br>avoid precluding future development of other areas, as well as ensuring<br>that the capacity of services has regard to the development potential of<br>neighbouring land in the Services Overlay.<br>In other cases, the area may be above the contour to which the Council<br>can supply water, where the landowner would have to provide their own<br>supply.  Such situations are a discretionary activity.  The Council wishes<br>to avoid a proliferation of small individual systems, and will be looking for<br>proposals that integrate with other developments, and have the ability to<br>serve a wider area.<br>There are also areas where services can be supplied, but where<br>additional conditions may apply eg. an additional financial contribution<br>towards the cost of a special water supply, or special conditions<br>regarding the point where the public supply may be accessed.<br>Refer to Policy DO14.3.1 Roading and DO14.3.2 Drainage, Water and<br>Utilities for direction in terms of when Council will fund infrastructure<br>provision to sites, or when the infrastructure provision shall be funded by<br>the developer.<br>The Council’s Infrastructure Division holds copies of maps which define<br>the servicing constraints in more detail.<br>Note: The capacity of the drain or wastewater network means the<br>capacity of the length of the drain from the site to, and including, its outfall<br>to a water body, coastal water or treatment facility.|

**A1-16** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


h) Carriageway widths may be varied from those given in Chapter 4 of the Nelson
Tasman Land Development Manual 2019, to allow the creation of open space or
planted areas within legal road, provided it can be demonstrated that traffic
movements will not be adversely affected. Compensatory parking bays may need to
be provided in suitable areas.
i) The need for earthworks and recontouring is recognised. However, this should
result in landforms which are visually sympathetic with the existing landforms.
Areas disturbed by earthworks should be restored to an appropriate shape, married
to the existing topography, and revegetated to soften their appearance.
j) Reserves and walkways should be sited with particular regard to providing public
views from the development, especially toward the surrounding hills and towards
the sea. Reserves and walkways should be linked with the road pattern to provide
an integrated open space framework in the development. In planning subdivisions,
sensitive landscapes and landforms should be incorporated within reserves.
k) Utilities and associated structures should be considered in planning the subdivision.
They should be sited, designed and installed in such a way that they do not detract
from the visual quality of the development or of distant views to the development.

### **AP7.4  performance guidelines - rural zone**


**AP7.4.1** **subdivision planning and development**


**AP7.4.1.i** Subdivision planning and development within the Landscape Overlay in the
Rural Zone should meet the following performance guidelines:


a) The subdivision should be planned and designed to reflect, and be in harmony with,
the forms, lines, textures, patterns and colours that exist on the site and in the
landscape context. Forms and lines come mainly from the landforms and their
edges. Textures, patterns and colour are derived mainly from the vegetation cover
and land use.
b) Subdivision should be planned and development carried out so that unnecessary
changes to landform or landscape features are avoided.
c) In planning the subdivision, sensitive landscapes and landforms should be placed in

reserves.
d) The number, shape, size, design and location of lots and building sites are to be
appropriate to the setting.
e) Significant natural and heritage features are to be recognised and protected or
enhanced.
g) Existing vegetation cover of value, both herbaceous and woody, should be retained
and protected.
h) The overall shape of the subdivision and allotment boundaries within it should
coincide with existing natural edges in the landscape, such as ridges, valleys and
streams.
i) Allotment shapes should be related to landform shapes, avoiding arbitrary edges
across landforms.
j) Allotments should be large enough to permit groupings of structures with large
areas of open unbuilt land of rural character between groups of structures.
k) Building sites are to be designated on the subdivision plan, and are to be sized,
shaped and located so that structures can be built with the apex of their rooflines
below the crests of ridges, in order to avoid being silhouetted against the sky.
l) Building sites should be located so that structures on them do not block views from
roads and public reserves.
m) Building sites are to be located so that road access can be provided to them, in an
alignment which is appropriate for the landscape and generally parallel with the
contours, with minimum earthworks and retaining structures and without steep
zigzags.
Roading is to be designed with a curving alignment that reflects the dominant lines
in the landscape context, and which generally parallels the existing contours. Steep
zigzags across the faces of slopes are to be avoided.
Roading should be planned and designed so that there are minimum cuts and fills
and no large retaining structures.


**Nelson Resource Management Plan (12/07/21)** **A7-3**


n) Reserves and walkways should be linked with the road pattern to provide an
integrated open space network through the subdivision.
o) Utilities should be sited, designed and installed in such a way that they do not
detract from the visual quality of the rural landscape, and should, wherever
possible, be placed underground.
p) Where utilities are placed above ground, their alignment should follow existing
contours and they should not be placed in situations where they will be silhouetted
against the sky.
q) Planning, design and development is to ensure that topography is restored to a
contour appropriate to the setting and which marries with adjacent slopes.
r) Appropriate herbaceous and/or woody vegetation cover is to be re-established on
all cuts, fills, backfilled trenches and other disturbed areas as part of the
subdivision planning and development.
s) Blocks of planting should be shaped to reflect existing forms on the site, avoiding
the imposition of arbitrary geometric shapes on slopes. Planting should be carried
out so that silhouetting of single specimens on the skyline is avoided.


**AP7.4.2** **structures in the rural zone**


**AP7.4.2.i** These guidelines are for residential and farm structures within the
Landscape Overlay in the Rural Zone.


Residential structures are considered to include: dwellings and associated outbuildings
such as garages and sheds, and small signs.


**AP7.4.2.ii** Farm structures are considered to include: barns, sheds, silos, yards, tanks,
tracks and small signs.


**AP7.4.2.iii** Structures are made visible in the landscape by their location and their form
and colour. Tracks are made visible by their location, and the line which results from
colour contrast with the background. Farm structures are often more noticeable in the
landscape because of their reflective surfaces and simple forms.


**AP7.4.2.iv** As controlled activities, the Council may exercise control over their
location, design and appearance.


**AP7.4.2.v** The purpose of these guidelines is to describe the management of the
characteristics of residential and farm structures that should be applied in order to
mitigate adverse visual effects in the Landscape Overlay.
a) Larger residential and farm structures should, as much as possible, be located on
the edges of landforms and vegetation patterns.
b) All residential and farm structures should be sited so that the apex of the roofline
does not project above the crest of the ridge and they should have a backdrop of
landform or vegetation.
c) In both residential and farm settings, associated structures should be clustered
together, and reflect unity of design in form and colour.
d) The forms of the structures should reflect background landforms, with moderatelysteep gabled and hip roofs, and variation in the line and form of wall surfaces.
Large unrelieved expanses of roofs and walls, and flat roofs should be avoided.
e) Colours of roofs and walls should be based on the background colours in the
landscape and be low in reflectivity, avoiding garish, unnatural colours. There can
be small areas of accent colour which contrast with the background, such as on
doors or window frames.
f) Signs should be for identification purposes only. They should be few in number and
small in size. They should be as low as possible and located so that they have a
backdrop of landform or vegetation immediately behind. The message area of the
sign should be in a colour derived from the landscape background.
g) Structures, such as yards, retaining walls and bridges should be of natural materials,
such as timber, in forms and lines appropriate for the landscape, and left to
weather naturally or finished with low reflectivity colours.
h) Tracks and access roads should, as much as possible, be located on the edges of
landform and vegetation patterns. They should have a curving alignment and
should run generally parallel with contours and on flatter areas. There should be
minimum cuts and fills. Avoid steep zigzag alignments.


**A7-4** **(01/09/04) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


# **appendix 10**
## **standards and** **terms for parking** **and loading**

### **AP10 introduction**

**AP10.i** This appendix deals with all requirements relating to parking and
loading.

### **AP10.1 application of appendix 10**


**AP10.1.i** Parking, loading and queuing must be provided and maintained on each
site in accordance with this appendix:


a) Where a building is constructed OR substantially reconstructed altered or added
to, or


b) Where it is proposed to construct, erect or alter one or more buildings or other
works in stages and the total proposed construction, erection or alteration would
if carried out other than in stages constitute a substantial reconstruction,
alteration or addition. Provided that stage works that occurred two calendar
years prior to the current proposal need not be counted, or


c) Where there is a change in the use of any land or building, or


d) When otherwise required by a rule in this Plan.

### **AP10.2 definitions**


**All weather surface**


means construction of a carriageway with adequate drainage, a sound subgrade and
compacted graded aggregates that results in a carriageway that is usable by vehicles in
all weather conditions.


**Note:** The expression “150mm compacted AP40 basecourse (min)” shall have the same
meaning as all weather surface. This expression should be used on plans and
specifications for developments requiring an all weather surface under this Plan.


**Classified Road**


means roads with a hierarchical classification of Arterial, Principal, and Collector.
Refer to Chapter 4 ‘Transport’ of the Nelson Tasman Land Development Manual 2019.


**Nelson Resource Management Plan (12/07/21)** **A10-1**


**Drive - Through Activities**


means an activity, other than a service station, where vehicles queue while awaiting
service, or an activity in which the customer obtains, gains access to or orders goods or
services from their vehicle and includes activities utilising vehicle control points such
as remote ordering points or service booths.


**Farming Activities**


means an activity for growing crops (including trees) or breeding or rearing live stock
animals and includes a hobby farm.
**Clarity Note:** For the purpose of this Appendix farm housing is classed as a residential
activity.


**Loading space**


means an on-site marked space where vehicles load or unload goods or people.


**Parking space**


means a useable, on-site, formed, marked space where vehicles can park.


**Permanent Surface**


means construction of a carriageway with adequate drainage, a sound subgrade and
compacted graded aggregates that results in a carriageway that is usable by vehicles in
all weather conditions.


**Professional Staff**


means medical practitioners, dentists, physiotherapists, practice nurses and any other
specialist employed at a health facility whose patients may consult for the provision of
medical advice independently. For the purpose of this definition, it includes veterinary
surgeons, but excludes medical receptionists and general nursing assistants.


**Road**


has the same meaning as in section 315 of the Local Government Act 1974 and includes
a motorway as defined in section 2 (1) of the Government Roading Powers Act 1989.


**Queuing Space**


means a useable, on-site, formed space, which complies with the relevant standards in
Appendix 9 & 10, where vehicles can stand while awaiting goods or services.


**Queuing Lane**


means an on-site vehicle access lane containing one or more queuing spaces, where
vehicles can queue one behind the other, in line, while awaiting goods or services.


**Unclassified Road**


means roads with a hierarchical classification of Sub-Collector, Local Roads and
Residential Lanes. Refer to Chapter 4 ‘Transport’ of the Nelson Tasman Land
Development Manual 2019.


**Unit**


In the case of Short Term Living Accommodation means:
a) a room for sleeping guests let for a single tariff (for example, in a studio motel,

hotel room, studio apartment, cabin), or a dormitory style room let to multiple
parties generally for individual tariffs (for example, some backpacker or hostel
accommodation), or
b) a collection of rooms for sleeping guests which form an entity and which are usually

let together for a single tariff (for example, a multi bedroom motel, hotel suite, or
apartment).


**Vehicle Parking Facilities**


means an activity where the service provided is parking for vehicles and includes
commercial car and truck parks and also includes parking ancillary to the predominant
activity, where the traffic flow or parking is controlled by means of a vehicle control
point such as a service booth or automated entry control device.


**A10-2** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**


### **AP10.3 calculation of coach loading and queuing spaces**

**AP10.3.i** Coach loading and queuing spaces must be provided as set out in
10.3.1, within the net area of every site at which the activity listed in 10.3.1 takes
place.


**AP10.3.ii** Whenever a building is increased in floor area, or undergoes a partial or
total change in activity, queuing requirements for the existing part of the building (if
any) or that part remaining in the existing activity, shall remain unaltered. In this case
the requirements relevant when the original activity, or part of it, shall continue to
apply. Queuing requirements for the increased floor area or that area with a new or
altered activity shall be calculated in accordance with Table 10.3.1.


**AP10.3.iii** Where the activity on the site involves more than one category of use
listed in 10.3.1, the number of on-site spaces required on that site will be the sum of
the requirements for each category.


When the assessment of the total number of queuing spaces results in a part space
being involved, any fraction under one half will be disregarded. Fractions of one half
or more will be counted as one parking space.






|Table 10.3.1 – coach loading|and queuing space requirements|
|---|---|
|**Activity**|**Coach Loading or Queuing Spaces Required**|

### **AP10.4 parking spaces for people with disabilities**

**AP10.4.i** The Building Act 2004 includes requirements for the provision of parking
for people with disabilities. The Building Act 2004 provisions must be complied with.
In effect, parking spaces may have to be designed in such a way that they are suitable
for disabled parking as required in the Building Act 2004.


**AP10.4.ii** These requirements apply when parking spaces are provided voluntarily
for an activity in accordance with ICr.31 or SCr.31.

### **AP10.5 dimensions - parking spaces and set down areas**


**AP10.5.i** Every parking space must be of a useable shape and condition and must
meet the following requirements:


a) An individual parking space must have the minimum dimensions of 3m wide and
5m long which provides sufficient space for the doors of a 85 percentile design car
to be opened to allow a person to enter or exit the design car, and


b) For two or more parking spaces (side by side) carparking spaces and access thereto
must comply with one of the two methods described below. Applicants must state
which of the two methods below has been used to achieve compliance:
i) Table 10.5.1 Method: Compliance with the parking layout provisions of Table
10.5.1 below, or
ii) 85 Percentile Car Method: Compliance with the 85 percentile car tracking
curve detailed in Appendix 12 (tracking curves). Provided these minimum
parking space dimensions must apply:
Side by Side Carparks: 5m long and 2.5m wide each, or
Parallel Carparks: 6m long and 2m wide each, and


c) The internal minimum height for any private parking space and access thereto
shall be at least 1.85m from the finished floor level and 2.3m from the finished
floor level for all other parking spaces available to the public.


**A10-4** **(12/07/21) Nelson Resource Management Plan**
